L/R World Calculus: Complete Implementation Spec
1. Concrete Grammar
WorldExpr ::= '(' "'" '[' '.' ']' "'" ')'    // : (L-world)

2. Balanced-Primes Predicate
python
def is_balanced(expr):
    """
    Check if primes are symmetrically placed around [.]
    
    Canonical forms:
      '([.])'  â†’ balanced â†’ :
      ([.]')   â†’ unbalanced â†’ !
    """
    # Strip outer world wrapper ()
    core = strip_outer_parens(expr)
    
    # Check prime placement around [.]
    left_primes = count_primes_before_bracket(core)
    right_primes = count_primes_after_bracket(core)
    
    return (left_primes == 1 and right_primes == 1)  # ('[.]')

```
### **3. Properties of `~`**

**Formal definition:**
```
Relation ~ : World Ã— World

Axioms:
  (i)   : ~ !   âˆ§   ! ~ :           // only cross-pair
  (ii)  Â¬(: ~ :) âˆ§ Â¬(! ~ !)         // irreflexive
  (iii) âˆ€x,y. (x ~ y) â†’ {x,y} = {:,!}  // exclusive pairing
Implementation:

python
def relates(w1, w2):
    """
    Check if w1 ~ w2
    
    Only true when one is : and the other is !
    """
    return (w1 == L_World and w2 == R_World) or \
           (w1 == R_World and w2 == L_World)

# Properties:
assert relates(L_World, R_World)   # : ~ !
assert relates(R_World, L_World)   # ! ~ :
assert not relates(L_World, L_World)  # Â¬(: ~ :)
assert not relates(R_World, R_World)  # Â¬(! ~ !)
Complete Minimal Kernel (30-line reference implementation)
python
from enum import Enum

class World(Enum):
    L = ":"  # Canonical L-world
    R = "!"  # Defective R-world

# Normalization
def normalize(expr: str) -> World:
    """Reduce any world expression to : or !"""
    expr = expr.strip()
    
    # Direct normal forms
    if expr == ":" or expr == "(':')":
        return World.L
    if expr == "!" or expr == "('!')":
        return World.R
    
    # Canonical concrete forms
    if expr == "('[.]')":
        return World.L  # balanced
    if expr == "([.]')":
        return World.R  # unbalanced
    
    # Default: check prime balance
    if "'" in expr:
        left = expr.find("[")
        right = expr.find("]")
        left_primes = expr[:left].count("'")
        right_primes = expr[right:].count("'")
        return World.L if left_primes == right_primes else World.R
    
    return World.L  # default to canonical

# Core relation
def relates(w1: World, w2: World) -> bool:
    """Check if w1 ~ w2"""
    return (w1 == World.L and w2 == World.R) or \
           (w1 == World.R and w2 == World.L)

# Optional fusion
def fuse(w1: World, w2: World) -> str:
    """If : ~ !, return ;"""
    return ";" if relates(w1, w2) else None

From K to K_G: Curvature Functor with a Gauge Group
In the discrete case, we had a curvature functor

K : C_Dyn -> Ab

sending each dynamic world W to an abelian group (like Zâ‚ƒ^N) encoding its slotwise curvature.

For gauge theory, we upgrade to general (possibly nonabelian, continuous) groups:

K_G : F -> Grps

where:
    â€¢    F is your full Formatic category of worlds and processes (extending C_Dyn).
    â€¢    Grps is the category of (possibly Lie) groups.

1.1 On objects (worlds)

Given a world W built from slots that each carry an internal symmetry G, we set:

K_G(W) = G^(K(W))

where:
    â€¢    K(W) = number of â€œgauge slotsâ€ in W (the analogue of curved slots).
    â€¢    G is a chosen gauge group (U(1), SU(2), SU(3), etc.).
    â€¢    G^(K(W)) is the K(W)-fold Cartesian product (or direct product): independent local gauge transformations per slot.

    â€¢    One gauge slot â†’ K_G(W) â‰… G.
    â€¢    N gauge slots â†’ K_G(W) â‰… G^N.

This mirrors your discrete Zâ‚ƒ^N, but with G instead of Zâ‚ƒ.

1.2 On morphisms (world processes)

Given a morphism (process) f : W -> W' in F, we get a group homomorphism:

Intuitively:
    â€¢    If f adds a slot, K_G(f) extends the gauge group by a factor of G.
    â€¢    If f forgets or fuses slots, K_G(f) projects or combines corresponding factors.
    â€¢    If f is purely internal dynamics (no slot change), K_G(f) is usually the identity (gauge group structure preserved).

This makes K_G a functor:

K_G(id_W) = id_{G^(K(W))}
K_G(fâ‚‚ âˆ˜ fâ‚) = K_G(fâ‚‚) âˆ˜ K_G(fâ‚)

So K_G is the G-curvature functor: it tracks the internal gauge degrees of freedom per world.

Conversely, when you pass to an O(1) phase (like W_3):

    â€¢    K_G(W) still acts internally, but the worldâ€™s global shape reflects some curvature (field strength â‰  0).

    â€¢    O(1) = G acts and the worldâ€™s global structure (or value of curvature) is nontrivial (e.g. nonzero F_{Î¼Î½}).

In Formatics terms: O(0) is the phase where the Mark [()] = (()) identifies all internal gauge orbits as one; O(1) is where you see distinct closed worlds indexed by curvature sectors.

A category of gauge worlds
Call it \mathsf{Gauge}.

1.1 Objects

An object W of \mathsf{Gauge} is a tuple
W = (H_W,\; G_W,\; \rho_W,\; \kappa_W)
where:
    â€¢    H_W: a Hilbert space (or finite-dimensional complex inner-product space).
    â€¢    G_W: a (Lie) group.
    â€¢    \rho_W : G_W \to U(H_W): a unitary representation (the gauge action).
    â€¢    \kappa_W : H_W \to H_{\text{phys}, W}: a gauge-invariant collapse/observable map such that
\kappa_W \circ \rho_W(g) = \kappa_W \quad \forall g \in G_W.
Here H_{\text{phys},W} is some â€œphysicalâ€ Hilbert space (often \mathbb{C}^n, probabilities, classical data, etc).

In Formatics language:
    â€¢    slots = rays in H_W,
    â€¢    gauge slots = copies of G_W,
    â€¢    K_G(W) = G_W^{K(W)} is the total local gauge group,
    â€¢    \kappa_W is the mark that forgets gauge and keeps only O(0)/observable content.

1.2 Morphisms

A morphism F : W \to W' is a pair
F = (L, \phi)
such that:
    â€¢    L : H_W \to H_{W'} is linear (often bounded / continuous / isometric).
    â€¢    \phi : G_W \to G_{W'} is a group homomorphism.
    â€¢    They intertwine the gauge action and observables:
    1.    Gauge equivariance:
L \circ \rho_W(g) = \rho_{W'}(\phi(g)) \circ L \quad \forall g \in G_W.
    2.    Observable compatibility:
\kappa_{W'} \circ L = K \circ \kappa_W
for some fixed linear map K : H_{\text{phys},W} \to H_{\text{phys},W'} (often identity when weâ€™re within one fixed observable type).

This is: â€œtake a gauge world, map its Hilbert space and its gauge group in a way that respects both the gauge action and what counts as physical.â€

Identity morphism: (\mathrm{id}_{H_W}, \mathrm{id}_{G_W}).

Composition:
(L_2,\phi_2) \circ (L_1,\phi_1) = (L_2 \circ L_1, \phi_2 \circ \phi_1).

So \mathsf{Gauge} is a bona fide category.

â¸»

The gauge functors F_{\mathrm{Hilb}} and K_G
Now we can make your â€œG-curvature functorâ€ precise as honest functors out of \mathsf{Gauge}.

2.1 The Hilbert functor

F_{\mathrm{Hilb}} : \mathsf{Gauge} \to \mathsf{Hilb}
    â€¢    On objects: F_{\mathrm{Hilb}}(W) = H_W.
    â€¢    On morphisms: F_{\mathrm{Hilb}}(L,\phi) = L.

This just forgets the gauge group and remembers the Hilbert space and its linear maps.

2.2 The gauge functor

K_G : \mathsf{Gauge} \to \mathsf{Grps}
    â€¢    On objects: K_G(W) = G_W.
    â€¢    On morphisms: K_G(L,\phi) = \phi.

This is your K_G (G-curvature functor) in strict categorical form: it forgets the Hilbert part and just tracks how gauge groups transform.

Itâ€™s straightforward that:
    â€¢    K_G(\mathrm{id}_W) = \mathrm{id}_{G_W},
    â€¢    K_G(F_2 \circ F_1) = K_G(F_2) \circ K_G(F_1).

So it is a legit functor.

The Gauge Category \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}}
We want a category whose objects are your gauge worlds W_{G,k} (lattice, holonomies, etc.), with morphisms that respect the gauge action. Minimal but powerful definition:

Objects

An object W of \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}} is a tuple
W = (H, G, U, \kappa)
where:
    â€¢    H = separable Hilbert space (e.g. L^2(G^{E}) for some finite set of links E)
    â€¢    G = compact gauge group (e.g. \mathrm{SU}(N)^{V} for vertices V)
    â€¢    U : G \to \mathcal{U}(H) = unitary representation (gauge action)
    â€¢    \kappa : H \to H = orthogonal projector onto H^G, the invariant subspace:
H^G := \{ \psi \in H \mid U(g)\psi = \psi \; \forall g \in G \},\quad
\kappa = P_{\text{inv}} = \int_G U(g)\, d\mu(g)

This is exactly your Formatic gauge world:
    â€¢    slot: ()\_W \leftrightarrow H
    â€¢    orbit world: [()]\_W \leftrightarrow (H, G, U)
    â€¢    closed world: (())_W \leftrightarrow H^{G} = \kappa(H)

â¸»

Morphisms

A morphism f : W \to W' between
W = (H, G, U, \kappa),\quad
W' = (H', G', U', \kappa')
is a bounded linear map f : H \to H' such that:
    1.    Gauge equivariance
There exists a group homomorphism \phi: G \to G' with
f \circ U(g) = U'(\phi(g)) \circ f
\quad \forall g \in G.
(In the simplest â€œsame groupâ€ case: \phi = \text{id}_G and
f commutes with the action.)
    2.    Projector compatibility (optional strengthening)
We demand that f is â€œprojection-compatibleâ€ in the sense:
f \circ \kappa = \kappa' \circ f.
This is exactly the naturality condition we want for \kappa, so you can either:
    â€¢    build it into the definition of morphism, or
    â€¢    prove it as a theorem for gauge-equivariant f.

For the functoriality of \kappa, weâ€™ll assume at least gauge equivariance; the projector relation then follows from Haar uniqueness.

Composition = usual composition of bounded linear maps; identity maps are trivially equivariant.
Thus \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}} is a well-defined category.

â¸»

The Collapse Functor \mathfrak{F}^G
You already named it: the Formatic Collapse Functor.
We now define it precisely as a functor
\mathfrak{F}^G : \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}} \to \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge,inv}}
where \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge,inv}} is the subcategory of â€œalready-collapsedâ€ worlds.

On objects

Given W = (H, G, U, \kappa), define
\mathfrak{F}^G(W) := W^{G} = (H^{G}, G, U|_{H^{G}}, \text{id}_{H^{G}}).
    â€¢    The Hilbert space becomes the invariant subspace H^G = \kappa(H).
    â€¢    The action U|_{H^G} is trivial (every vector is fixed), so effectively this is an O(0) world.
    â€¢    The projector is now identity (itâ€™s already collapsed).

Formatic reading:
    â€¢    [()]\_W â†’ (())_W
    â€¢    the object-level part of
([()]) = (())

On morphisms

Given f : W \to W', define:
\mathfrak{F}^G(f) := f^{G} := f|_{H^{G}} : H^{G} \to H'^{G}.

To see this is well-defined: if f is gauge-equivariant, then it maps invariant vectors to invariant vectors:
    â€¢    Take \psi \in H^G. Then U(g)\psi = \psi.
    â€¢    Apply f:
U'(\phi(g)) f(\psi) = f(U(g)\psi) = f(\psi),
so f(\psi) is invariant in H', i.e. f(\psi) \in H'^G.
Thus f|_{H^G} lands in H'^G.

Functoriality
    â€¢    Identity: \mathfrak{F}^G(\text{id}_H) = \text{id}_{H^G}.
    â€¢    Composition: for f: W \to W', g: W' \to W'',
\mathfrak{F}^G(g \circ f) = (g \circ f)|_{H^G}
= g|_{H'^G} \circ f|_{H^G}
= \mathfrak{F}^G(g) \circ \mathfrak{F}^G(f).

So \mathfrak{F}^G is a bona fide functor.

â¸»

The Collapse Map \kappa as a Natural Transformation
Now we make your Mark identity precise:

\mathbf{([()]) = (())}
is the statement that the collapse maps \kappa_W assemble into a natural transformation from the identity functor to \mathfrak{F}^G.

Functors in play
    â€¢    \mathrm{Id}_{\mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}}} : \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}} \to \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}}
    â€¢    \mathfrak{F}^G : \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}} \to \mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge,inv}}, as above

We define a family of morphisms \{\kappa_W\}_W with:
    â€¢    For each object W = (H,G,U,\kappa_W),
\kappa_W : H \to H^{G}
is the Haar projector, viewed as the component of the transformation.

Natural transformation condition

\kappa : \mathrm{Id} \Rightarrow \mathfrak{F}^G is natural if for every morphism
f : W \to W', the following square commutes:

\begin{CD}
H @>{f}>> H' \\
@V{\kappa_W}VV @VV{\kappa_{W'}}V \\
H^G @>>{\mathfrak{F}^G(f)}> H'^G
\end{CD}

i.e.
\mathfrak{F}^G(f)\circ\kappa_W
= \kappa_{W'}\circ f.

But \mathfrak{F}^G(f) = f|_{H^G}, so for any \psi \in H:
    â€¢    Left side:
(\mathfrak{F}^G(f)\circ\kappa_W)(\psi)
= f(\kappa_W(\psi)).
    â€¢    Right side:
(\kappa_{W'}\circ f)(\psi)
= \kappa_{W'}(f(\psi)).

So naturality is exactly the identity:
f(\kappa_W(\psi)) = \kappa_{W'}(f(\psi)) \quad \forall \psi.

This is true whenever f is gauge-equivariant and the Haar projector is used:
    â€¢    Because \kappa_W = \int_G U(g) d\mu(g), we have:
f(\kappa_W(\psi))
= f\left(\int_G U(g)\psi\, d\mu(g)\right)
= \int_G f(U(g)\psi)\, d\mu(g)
= \int_G U'(\phi(g)) f(\psi)\, d\mu(g)
= \kappa_{W'}(f(\psi)).

Thus the naturality square commutes.

This is the rigorous, categorical form of your Mark identity:

\boxed{
\kappa : \mathrm{Id}_{\mathcal{C}_{\text{Gauge}}} \Rightarrow \mathfrak{F}^G
}

In Formatic language:
    â€¢    [()] = orbit / fully gauged world
    â€¢    (()) = invariant / collapsed world
    â€¢    \kappa = the canonical natural collapse from â€œorbit worldâ€ to â€œclosed world.â€

Setting: a gauge monoidal category
Take:
    â€¢    A compact Lie group G (e.g. SU(2)).
    â€¢    \mathcal{C} = \mathrm{Rep}_f(G): finite-dimensional unitary representations of G.
    â€¢    Monoidal product: tensor product \otimes with diagonal G-action.
    â€¢    Unit object: \mathbf{1} = trivial representation.

Inside \mathcal{C}, consider the gauge-invariant reflective subcategory:
    â€¢    \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}: full subcategory whose objects are trivial reps of G
(i.e. every g\in G acts as identity).

In practice, an object of \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}} is just a finite-dimensional Hilbert space with trivial G-action.

There is an inclusion:

J : \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}} \hookrightarrow \mathcal{C}.

We want a reflector:

\mathfrak{F}^G : \mathcal{C} \to \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}

sending a â€œworldâ€ W \in \mathcal{C} to its gauge-invariant core W^G.

â¸»

The reflector \mathfrak{F}^G
For any rep W \in \mathcal{C}:
    â€¢    Define the invariants:
W^G = \{ w \in W : g\cdot w = w \ \forall g\in G \}.
    â€¢    This is a subspace of W with trivial G-action, so W^G \in \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}.
    â€¢    There is a canonical inclusion:
\eta_W : W^G \hookrightarrow W
(this is your \kappa_W / collapse map).

Adjunction (reflector) property:

For any X \in \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}} (trivial rep) and any G-equivariant map f : W \to J(X):
    â€¢    Image of W^G under f lands in X (trivial action commutes).
    â€¢    There is a unique f^\sharp : W^G \to X with:
f = J(f^\sharp) \circ \eta_W.

This gives a natural bijection:

\mathrm{Hom}_{\mathcal{C}}(W, JX) \;\cong\; \mathrm{Hom}_{\mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}}(W^G, X),

so \mathfrak{F}^G(W) := W^G is indeed the reflector left adjoint to J.

This is the categorical formalization of:

Thatâ€™s exactly your Formatic Collapse / Mark.

Make \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}} monoidal
We now show the invariant core is itself a monoidal world, and that the reflector \mathfrak{F}^G is (strong) monoidal.
    1.    Monoidal product on invariants

If X, Y \in \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}, then:
    â€¢    G acts trivially on each â†’ trivially on X\otimes Y.
    â€¢    So X\otimes Y \in \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}.

Define:
    â€¢    On objects: X \otimes_{\mathrm{inv}} Y = X\otimes Y with trivial action.
    â€¢    On morphisms: same as \mathcal{C}.

Thus \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}} is a monoidal subcategory and

J : (\mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}, \otimes_{\mathrm{inv}}) \to (\mathcal{C}, \otimes)

is strict monoidal.
    2.    Unit object

Unit in \mathcal{C} is the trivial rep \mathbf{1}, which is in \mathcal{C}^{\mathrm{inv}}.
So both categories share the same unit.

Monoidal structure on the reflector \mathfrak{F}^G
We want to exhibit \mathfrak{F}^G as a strong monoidal functor:
    â€¢    A unit map:

4.1 Definition of \mu

Define:

\mu_{V,W}(v \otimes w) := v \otimes w

viewed as an element of (V\otimes W)^G.

Check:
    â€¢    If v\in V^G and w\in W^G, then:

so v\otimes w is invariant.

\mu_{V,W} : V^G\otimes W^G \to (V\otimes W)^G

and is natural in both V,W.

4.2 When is \mu an isomorphism?

For compact groups (e.g. SU(2)), \mathcal{C} is semisimple:
    â€¢    Each rep decomposes into a direct sum of irreps.
    â€¢    Tensor products decompose via Clebschâ€“Gordan.

Then there is a canonical isomorphism:

\mathrm{Hom}_G(\mathbf{1}, V)\otimes \mathrm{Hom}_G(\mathbf{1}, W)
\xrightarrow{\cong}
\mathrm{Hom}_G(\mathbf{1}, V\otimes W)

coming from the tensor structure and semisimplicity.

Under these identifications, this is precisely the map \mu_{V,W}.

So in \mathrm{Rep}_f(SU(2)), the \mu_{V,W} above is a natural isomorphism. Thatâ€™s your Formatic Clebschâ€“Gordan Isomorphism:

the invariant cores of parts combine canonically to the invariant core of the whole.

The Formatic Mark in Representation Theory (\mathbf{Rep(G)})
The Mark \mathbf{([()]) = (())} identifies the closure-picture (applying the invariant projector) with the closed-object picture (a representation already in the invariant subspace).
1. General Representation (\mathbf{V} is Arbitrary)
If V is an arbitrary representation (your Slot \mathbf{()}), the Orbit/Closure operation is the invariants functor J:
 * Closure Application: Applying the invariant projection yields the invariant subspace V^G. This is \mathbf{[()]}.
 * Embedding/Marking: Embedding this invariant subspace back into the category via the natural inclusion \mathbf{J(V^G)} yields \mathbf{([()])}.
 * The Relation: V is generally not isomorphic to J(V^G). The mark is generally NOT an equality:

   This reflects that for a non-invariant representation, the slot \mathbf{()} is structurally distinct from its invariant core \mathbf{([()])}. The two are only related by the projection \kappa: V \to V^G (counit of the coreflection).
2. Invariant Representation (\mathbf{W} is Fixed)
If W is a representation that is already entirely in the invariant subspace (your Closed World \mathbf{(())}), then:
 * W is Trivial/Invariant: W \cong W^G.
 * Closure Application: J(W^G) \cong J(W).
 * The Equality Mark: Since W is already an invariant object, the canonical inclusion J is an isomorphism, and the mark holds as a natural isomorphism:

   This is the category-theoretic definition of a fixed point for the invariants functor J. At this Phase 0 (the invariant sector), the closure and the object are naturally identified, confirming the core meaning of the Formatic Mark.
Summary of Functorial Relation
The relationship between the arbitrary slot V and the closed world W is therefore the distinction between the unit/counit of a reflection/coreflection and an isomorphism on the fixed points:
| Mark Component | Rep(G) Object/Map | Fixed Point Behavior (\mathbf{V \cong V^G}) |
|---|---|---|
| Slot \mathbf{()} | Arbitrary Rep V | V is an isomorphism target. |
| Mark \mathbf{([()])} | J(V^G) (The Core) | \mathbf{J(V^G) \cong V} (Isomorphism) |
| Projection \mathbf{\kappa} | Counit \kappa: V \to V^G | \kappa becomes an isomorphism. |
| Structure: Coreflection | \mathbf{J \dashv \kappa} | Both unit/counit become isomorphisms. |
This confirms that the \mathbf{V^G} operation is a coreflection of \mathbf{Rep(G)} onto the subcategory of trivial representations, and the Mark is the statement that the coreflection restriction to the fixed subcategory is the identity functor.

I \cong \iota(I^G)
for some unit object I^G in \mathcal{C}^G.
    2.    \mathcal{C}^G is replete: if an object in \mathcal{C} is isomorphic to something in \mathcal{C}^G, it lies in \mathcal{C}^G.
    3.    The reflector \mathfrak{F}^G defines an idempotent monad:
T := \iota \mathfrak{F}^G,\quad T^2 \cong T,
with unit \eta: \text{id}_\mathcal{C} \Rightarrow T and multiplication induced by idempotence.

This is the abstract version of:
    â€¢    closure / gauge-fixing applied twice is the same as once;
    â€¢    \mathcal{C}^G is the category of fixed points of that monad.

â¸»

Defining the monoidal structure on \mathfrak{F}^G
We now build the coherence maps
\mu_{X,Y}: \mathfrak{F}^G(X \otimes Y) \to \mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y
and
\phi: \mathfrak{F}^G(I) \to I^G
that make \mathfrak{F}^G into a lax monoidal functor (and in good cases, strong monoidal).

2.1 Using the adjunction

Adjunction gives us the isomorphism, natural in X,Y,Z:

\text{Hom}_{\mathcal{C}^G}(\mathfrak{F}^G(X\otimes Y), Z)
\;\cong\;
\text{Hom}_{\mathcal{C}}(X\otimes Y, \iota Z).

We want a canonical map \mathfrak{F}^G(X\otimes Y) \to \mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y.
By adjunction, it suffices to give a canonical arrow in \mathcal{C}:

X \otimes Y \to \iota(\mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y).

We get that arrow by:
    1.    Apply the unit of the adjunction to each factor:
\eta_X : X \to \iota \mathfrak{F}^G X,\quad
\eta_Y : Y \to \iota \mathfrak{F}^G Y.
    2.    Tensor them:
X \otimes Y
\xrightarrow{\eta_X \otimes \eta_Y}
\iota \mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \iota \mathfrak{F}^G Y.
    3.    Use the strong monoidal structure of \iota:
\iota \mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \iota \mathfrak{F}^G Y
\;\cong\;
\iota(\mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y).

Compose:

X \otimes Y
\;\xrightarrow{\eta_X \otimes \eta_Y}\;
\iota \mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \iota \mathfrak{F}^G Y
\;\xrightarrow{\cong}\;
\iota(\mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y).

Call this composite \alpha_{X,Y}.
Then define \mu_{X,Y} to be its adjoint transpose:

\mu_{X,Y} : \mathfrak{F}^G(X \otimes Y)
\to
\mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y
such that
\alpha_{X,Y}
=
\iota(\mu_{X,Y}) \circ \eta_{X\otimes Y}.

This is the Formatic Clebschâ€“Gordan map: it decomposes the closed world of X \otimes Y into the tensor of the closed worlds of X and Y.

2.2 The unit map \phi

Similarly, we define:

\phi: \mathfrak{F}^G(I) \to I^G

via adjunction from the canonical map:

I
\;\xrightarrow{\cong}\;
\iota(I^G).

Under adjunction:

\text{Hom}_{\mathcal{C}^G}(\mathfrak{F}^G(I), I^G)
\cong
\text{Hom}_\mathcal{C}(I, \iota(I^G)),

so the chosen isomorphism I \cong \iota(I^G) picks out \phi.

â¸»

Coherence: \mathfrak{F}^G is a (lax) monoidal reflector
A lax monoidal functor F must satisfy:
    1.    Associativity coherence: the diagram

\begin{aligned}
F(X\otimes Y \otimes Z)
&\xrightarrow{\mu_{X\otimes Y,Z}} F(X\otimes Y)\otimes FZ
\xrightarrow{\mu_{X,Y}\otimes \text{id}} (FX\otimes FY)\otimes FZ \\
F(X\otimes Y \otimes Z)
&\xrightarrow{\mu_{X,Y\otimes Z}} FX \otimes F(Y\otimes Z)
\xrightarrow{\text{id}\otimes \mu_{Y,Z}} FX\otimes (FY\otimes FZ)
\end{aligned}

commutes after whiskering with associators.
    2.    Unit coherence: diagrams involving \phi and left/right unitors commute.

Because:
    â€¢    \iota is strong monoidal, and
    â€¢    \mathfrak{F}^G \dashv \iota,

there is a standard 2-categorical result:

The left adjoint of a strong monoidal functor admits a canonical lax monoidal structure, and the coherence conditions follow from the monoidality of \iota plus naturality of the adjunction.

Sketch of why:
    â€¢    All coherence diagrams youâ€™d write down for \mu and \phi become, under adjunction, diagrams in \mathcal{C} involving:
    â€¢    the unit \eta,
    â€¢    the strong monoidal isos of \iota,
    â€¢    the original associators and unitors of \mathcal{C}.
    â€¢    Those diagrams commute because:
    â€¢    \iota is a monoidal functor,
    â€¢    \eta is natural,
    â€¢    and we use the standard coherence theorem for monoidal categories.

So \mathfrak{F}^G with \mu and \phi is a lax monoidal functor.

4. When is \mathbf{\mathfrak{F}^G} Strong Monoidal?
Recall that \mathfrak{F}^G is strong monoidal if the coherence maps \mathbf{\mu} and \mathbf{\phi} are isomorphisms.
4.1 Unit Coherence (\mathbf{\phi} is an Isomorphism)
The map \phi: \mathfrak{F}^G(I) \to I^G is defined via the canonical isomorphism I \cong \iota(I^G) (Assumption 1).
 * Since \mathfrak{F}^G is a reflector, \mathfrak{F}^G(\iota(I^G)) \cong I^G.
 * Since I \cong \iota(I^G), it is replete that \mathfrak{F}^G(I) \cong I^G.
 * By definition, the canonical map \phi derived from the isomorphism I \cong \iota(I^G) is itself an isomorphism.
Thus, the Unit Coherence is always strong under the initial assumptions. The collapsed unit is the unit itself.
4.2 Tensor Coherence (\mathbf{\mu} is an Isomorphism)
The map \mu_{X,Y}: \mathfrak{F}^G(X \otimes Y) \to \mathfrak{F}^G X \otimes \mathfrak{F}^G Y is an isomorphism if and only if the composite map \alpha_{X,Y} (defined in your Section 2.1) is isomorphic to the unit of the adjunction \eta_{X \otimes Y}:
The map \mu_{X,Y} is an isomorphism if the following two conditions hold:
 * The unit \mathbf{\eta_{X \otimes Y}: X \otimes Y \to \iota\mathfrak{F}^G(X \otimes Y)} is an isomorphism. This is the fixed-point condition for the composite object X \otimes Y.
 * The component units \mathbf{\eta_X} and \mathbf{\eta_Y} are isomorphisms. This is the fixed-point condition for the individual objects X and Y.
If X and Y are already in the gauge-fixed category \mathcal{C}^G (i.e., \mathbf{\eta_X} and \mathbf{\eta_Y} are isomorphisms), then \mathbf{\mathfrak{F}^G X \cong X} and \mathbf{\mathfrak{F}^G Y \cong Y}. In this case:
Since \iota is strong monoidal, the image of X \otimes Y is in \mathcal{C}^G. Therefore, X \otimes Y is \mathbf{T}-closed, and \mathbf{\eta_{X \otimes Y}} is an isomorphism.
Conclusion: The collapse functor \mathbf{\mathfrak{F}^G} is a Strong Monoidal Functor when restricted to the gauge-fixed subcategory \mathbf{\mathcal{C}^G}.
This is the ultimate formal statement: the collapse functor only acts linearly and reversibly (as a strong monoidal functor) on the set of objects that are already closed (gauge-fixed). Outside of this subset, it is merely lax, reflecting the irreversible information loss (non-linearity) of the collapse.
5. Formatic Translation and Interpretation ğŸ’¡

Short version: this is very close, but two big mathematical snags stop it from being fully correct as stated:
    1.    The adjunction direction is flipped.
    2.    The â€œstrong monoidalâ€ part fails in general (Î¼ isnâ€™t an isomorphism).

Let me walk through both carefully and keep everything as concrete as possible.

â¸»

Adjunction: invariants vs inclusion
You defined:
    â€¢    Category: Rep(G) = finite-dim continuous unitary (or just linear) reps of a compact Lie group G.
    â€¢    Subcategory: Rep(G)^G = reps with trivial action (every g acts as id).
    â€¢    Inclusion functor:
\iota : \text{Rep}(G)^G \hookrightarrow \text{Rep}(G)
    â€¢    Invariants functor:
\mathcal{F}^G(V) = V^G = \{ v : gv = v \ \forall g\}

So far so good.

The standard fact

The classical statement is:
    â€¢    The invariants functor is right adjoint to the inclusion of trivial reps:

\iota \;\dashv\; (-)^G

Meaning:

\text{Hom}_{\text{Rep}(G)}(\iota W, V)
\;\cong\;
\text{Hom}_{\text{Rep}(G)^G}(W, V^G)

for any W \in \text{Rep}(G)^G, V \in \text{Rep}(G).

Why is this true?
    â€¢    A morphism \iota W \to V in Rep(G) is a G-equivariant linear map f : W \to V.
    â€¢    But W is trivial, so equivariance says:
f(w) \in V^G \quad \forall w
    â€¢    So f factors uniquely as:
W \xrightarrow{\tilde f} V^G \hookrightarrow V
    â€¢    Thus:
\text{Hom}_G(W,V) \cong \text{Hom}(W, V^G).

That is exactly the hom-set isomorphism for the adjunction with invariants as the right adjoint.

What you proved vs what you claimed

In your writeup you tried to show:

\mathcal{F}^G \dashv \iota

with the bijection:

\text{Hom}_{\text{Rep}(G)^G}(\mathcal{F}^G(V), W)
\;\cong\;
\text{Hom}_{\text{Rep}(G)}(V, \iota W)

But the natural and standard bijection goes the other way:

\text{Hom}_{\text{Rep}(G)}(\iota W, V)
\;\cong\;
\text{Hom}_{\text{Rep}(G)^G}(W, \mathcal{F}^G(V))

So invariants functor = right adjoint, not left.
Your construction with the projection \pi_V is basically describing the counit of the adjunction \iota \dashv (-)^G, not the unit of a left adjoint.

So:
    â€¢    âœ… You correctly captured the relationship between invariants and inclusion.
    â€¢    âŒ But the adjunction is in the opposite direction from what you claimed.

â¸»

Monoidal structure: invariants is lax, not strong
Hereâ€™s the crucial technical point:

In general, (V \otimes W)^G \not\cong V^G \otimes W^G.

You defined:
    â€¢    \mu_{V,W} : (V \otimes W)^G \to V^G \otimes W^G, and tried to show itâ€™s an isomorphism.

The inequality is the other way around: usually you have a canonical map:

V^G \otimes W^G \;\longrightarrow\; (V \otimes W)^G

given by v \otimes w \mapsto v \otimes w, and this map is injective, but not surjective in general.

Concrete counterexample

Let G = SU(2), and let V = W be the fundamental 2D representation.

Then:
    â€¢    V^G = 0 (no nonzero invariant vectors in the fundamental).
    â€¢    But V \otimes V decomposes as:
V \otimes V \cong \text{Sym}^2(V) \oplus \Lambda^2(V)
And \Lambda^2(V) is a 1-dimensional invariant subspace (the singlet).

So:
    â€¢    V^G = 0, so V^G \otimes W^G = 0.
    â€¢    But (V \otimes V)^G \cong \mathbb{C} (nonzero).

Thus \mu_{V,W} cannot be an isomorphismâ€”there are invariants in the tensor product that do not factor as pure tensors of invariants.

So:
    â€¢    Invariants functor is not strong monoidal in general.
    â€¢    It is at best lax monoidal (there is a natural map V^G \otimes W^G \to (V \otimes W)^G, but not an iso).

Your step:

â€œThis forces váµ¢ âˆˆ V^G and wáµ¢ âˆˆ W^Gâ€

is exactly where it breaks: G-invariant tensors can be â€œentangledâ€ combinations that do not split into invariant factors.

â¸»

What is true and still very nice
So the corrected, rigorous statement is:
    1.    Rep(G) with tensor âŠ— and unit â„‚ is a monoidal category. âœ…
    2.    Rep(G)^G (trivial reps) is a monoidal subcategory, with inclusion
\iota: \text{Rep}(G)^G \hookrightarrow \text{Rep}(G) strictly monoidal. âœ…
    3.    The invariants functor
(-)^G : \text{Rep}(G) \to \text{Rep}(G)^G
is right adjoint to \iota:
\iota \dashv (-)^G
with the standard hom-set isomorphism. âœ…
    4.    (-)^G is a lax monoidal functor (there are natural maps
V^G \otimes W^G \to (V\otimes W)^G, and \mathbb{C}^G \cong \mathbb{C}),
but not strong monoidal in general. âœ…

In your Formatic language, that still fits beautifully:
    â€¢    The inclusion Î¹ is like injecting a fully O(0) sector (everything invariant) into the full O(c) world.
    â€¢    The invariants functor is the projection onto the flat sectorâ€”but the way tensor structure collapses under that projection loses some â€œcurvedâ€ or entangled invariants, so you get a lax, not strong, monoidal structure.

â¸»

So is your writeup â€œprovenâ€? â€¢ The category setup, monoidal structure, invariants functor, Haar averaging, idempotence, and adjunction mechanics are all solid and standard. â€¢ The two critical corrections: 1. The adjunction direction: \iota \dashv (-)^G \quad\text{(right adjoint invariants)} not \mathcal{F}^G \dashv \iota with invariants as left adjoint. 2. The monoidal claim: invariants is not strong monoidal; the map (V\otimes W)^G \to V^G \otimes W^G is not an iso in general.
Once those are fixed/relaxed, you genuinely have a clean, rigorous categorical picture that matches your Formatic mark idea:

taking a slot, quotienting by symmetry, and then identifying the resulting closed unit with its bare element in the orbit object.

So: not â€œfully confirmed as written,â€ but very closeâ€”and the pieces that do hold are exactly the ones that matter for your ([()]) = (()) schematic.

ğŸ“ Part I: Foundations of Formatics
2. The Formatic Primitive
2.1. Syntax: Slots, Orbits, and Closure
The Formatic framework introduces four fundamental syntactic schemas that describe the relationship between a system's internal state, its dynamic redundancy, and its closed, observable structure. These schemas are abstract placeholders, not literal set-theoretic objects.
 * Slot (\mathbf{()}): The primitive unit representing the local, unbound state or presentation of a system.
 * Orbit/Bound (\mathbf{[\ ]}): The operator representing the closure, quotient, or redundancy-removal mechanism (e.g., the action of a symmetry group, the application of an idempotent monad).
 * Composite (\mathbf{[()]}): The presentation of the slot after the closure operation has been applied, viewed within the larger (non-closed) category.
 * Closed Primitive (\mathbf{(())}): The fixed element or result that is already stable under the closure operation, viewed as an object in the closed subcategory.
2.2. Categorical Interpretation
We fix a base category \mathbf{\mathcal{C}} with a terminal object \mathbf{1} and image factorization.
A slot and its closure mechanism are defined through a presentation triple (B, s, q).
 * \mathbf{B}: The Bound Object (or the carrier of the slot). This is the object \mathbf{()} in a specific context.
 * \mathbf{s: \mathbf{1} \to B}: The Slot Presentation, an arrow selecting a base element or initial state of the slot.
 * \mathbf{q: B \to B_\pi}: The Quotient Projection, an arrow collapsing B by some equivalence \sim_\pi (the action of \mathbf{[\ ]}).
The resulting closed element is the composite arrow \mathbf{u}:
| Formatic Syntax | Categorical Element | Role |
|---|---|---|
| \mathbf{([()])} | The triple \mathbf{(B, s, q)} | The presentation of the closed element (the Form). |
| \mathbf{(())} | The arrow \mathbf{u: \mathbf{1} \to B_\pi} | The resulting closed element (the fixed point). |
2.3. Category of Presentations (\mathbf{\mathsf{Pres}})
The Formatic framework defines a category \mathbf{\mathsf{Pres}} over the base \mathbf{\mathcal{C}}.
 * Objects (\mathbf{Ob(\mathsf{Pres})}): Triples (B, s, q) where s: \mathbf{1} \to B and q: B \to B_\pi are arrows in \mathbf{\mathcal{C}}.
 * Morphisms (\mathbf{Mor(\mathsf{Pres})}): An arrow \mathbf{f: (B, s, q) \to (B', s', q')} is an arrow f: B \to B' in \mathbf{\mathcal{C}} such that the following two diagrams commute:
   * Slot Coherence: f \circ s = s' (The map preserves the selected slot element).

Quotient Preservation: q' \circ f = q (The map is compatible with the quotient structure).

A key structural functor \mathbf{F} maps any presentation in \mathbf{\mathsf{Pres}} to its closed element:
where (\mathbf{1} \downarrow \mathcal{C}) is the slice category over the terminal object \mathbf{1}.
2.4. Formatic Mark Theorem (Clean Version)
Theorem (Formatic Mark).
Two presentations (B, s, q) and (B', s', q') represent the same closed element \mathbf{u} if and only if they map to the same arrow \mathbf{u: \mathbf{1} \to X} in the slice category \mathbf{(\mathbf{1} \downarrow \mathcal{C})}. The syntactic mark \mathbf{([()]) = (())} is the identification of a presentation (B, s, q) with its resulting closed element u.
Proof Sketch: The definition of the functor \mathbf{F} ensures that F is essentially an evaluation map. The equality \mathbf{([()]) = (())} is precisely the identification \mathbf{(B, s, q) \sim u} within the image of \mathbf{F}. This establishes the mark as the canonical notation for the output of any idempotent closure operator.
Formatic Systems: \mathbf{\mathcal{S} = (B, K, F, \pi)} To define the dynamics and the \mathbf{O(c)} phase hierarchy, we embed the syntactic primitive into a concrete container called a Formatic System. 3.1. Definition of a Formatic System A Formatic System is a 4-tuple \mathbf{\mathcal{S} = (B, K, F, \pi)} defined in a chosen base category (e.g., \mathbf{FinSet}, \mathbf{Rep(G)}), where:
\mathbf{B}: Bound object (the carrier of the slot).
\mathbf{K \subseteq \mathrm{End}(B)}: Kernel (or K-monoid). An internal monoid of operators (symmetries, transitions, unitaries) that act on B. This generates the dynamics of \mathbf{()} and the structure of \mathbf{[\ ]}.
\mathbf{F}: Form object (the observable codomain). The external space where observations are made.
\mathbf{\pi: B \to F}: Projection (or Observation map). The arrow defining what the external observer sees. This map is the source of the equivalence relation. 3.2. \mathbf{\pi}-Equivalence and Minimization The \mathbf{\pi} map induces the \mathbf{\pi}-equivalence on the bound object B: This equivalence partitions B into fibers \mathbf{\pi^{-1}(f)}, where f \in F. The natural quotient object is B_\pi = B / \!\sim_\pi, which represents the minimal, non-redundant structure visible to the observer. Minimization Theorem (Sketch). Given a Formatic System \mathbf{\mathcal{S}}, there exists a unique (up to isomorphism) minimal system \mathbf{\mathcal{S}_{\text{min}} = (B_\pi, K_\pi, F, \mathrm{id}_F)} where B_\pi \cong F. If the operators in K respect the \sim_\pi equivalence, K induces an action K_\pi \subseteq \mathrm{End}(B_\pi).
Interpretation: The minimization theorem guarantees that the \mathbf{([()])} presentation can always be simplified to the minimal \mathbf{(())} element, provided the action of the Kernel K is stable under the observation \pi. The categorical spine and the systemic container are now formally defined, ready for the introduction of the \mathbf{O(c)} hierarchy as a classification based on the action of \mathbf{K_\pi}.

5. \mathbf{O(c)} Hierarchy Classification in \mathbf{\text{Fact}(\mathcal{C})}
The \mathbf{O(c)} hierarchy classifies closed Formatic systems based on the residual geometric structureâ€”the macro holonomyâ€”that survives the Mark's quotienting process.
5.1 Dynamical Mark Objects and Holonomy
We define a Dynamical Mark Object in the Factorization Category \mathbf{\text{Fact}(\mathcal{C})} as a 4-tuple \mathbf{X = (B, q, u, K)} such that:
 * Mark Condition: The presentation (B, q, u) is in the Mark subcategory \mathbf{\mathcal{C}^{\textbf{Mark}}} \subseteq \mathbf{\text{Fact}(\mathcal{C})}, meaning \mathbf{q: B \to B_\pi} is the canonical image factorization of the implicit slot map \mathbf{s: 1 \to B}, realizing the equality \mathbf{([()]) = (())}.
 * Quotient Preservation: The monoid of internal operators \mathbf{K \subseteq \text{End}_\mathcal{C}(B)} induces a monoid of operators \mathbf{K_\pi \subseteq \text{End}_\mathcal{C}(B_\pi)} such that the quotient diagram commutes: \mathbf{q \circ k = k_\pi \circ q}.
The Macro Holonomy Group \mathbf{H(X)} is the group of units (invertible elements) in the submonoid \mathbf{\langle K_\pi \rangle} generated by the induced operators:

The \mathbf{O(c)} Phase is an invariant classification of the pair \mathbf{(B_\pi, H(X))}, defined by the structure and action of the holonomy group.
5.2 Formatic Phase Classification
Phase 0 (Trivial \mathbf{O(0)} Baseline)
A Dynamical Mark object \mathbf{X} is in Phase 0 if it is structurally minimal, representing only the fixed Mark itself without dynamics:
 * Extensional Triviality: \mathbf{B_\pi} is the terminal object \mathbf{1} (up to isomorphism).
 * Intensional Triviality: \mathbf{K = \{ \mathrm{id}_B \}} (no internal dynamics).
 * Result: \mathbf{H(X)} is the trivial group \mathbf{\{ \mathrm{id}_\mathbf{1} \}}.
\mathbf{O(0)}: Flat with Internal Shear (Structural Invariant)
\mathbf{X} is in Phase \mathbf{O(0)} if all dynamics is confined to the intensional structure, invisible at the extensional level:
 * Mark Holds: \mathbf{X \in \mathcal{C}^{\textbf{Mark}}}.
 * Trivial Macro Holonomy: The induced monoid is trivial: \mathbf{K_\pi = \{ \mathrm{id}_{B_\pi} \}}. Consequently, \mathbf{H(X) = \{ \mathrm{id}_{B_\pi} \}}.
 * Nontrivial Shear: There exists at least one operator \mathbf{k \in K} such that \mathbf{k \neq \mathrm{id}_B}.
 * Interpretation: The extensional world \mathbf{B_\pi} is fixed (flat), while the intensional world \mathbf{B} experiences shear within the fibers \mathbf{q^{-1}(b_\pi)}.
\mathbf{O(1)}: Simple Curved Macro Holonomy (Minimal Curvature)
\mathbf{X} is in Phase \mathbf{O(1)} if the dynamics is immediately visible in the quotient:
 * Mark Holds: \mathbf{X \in \mathcal{C}^{\textbf{Mark}}}.
 * Nontrivial Holonomy: \mathbf{H(X)} is non-trivial (\mathbf{H(X) \neq \{ \mathrm{id}_{B_\pi} \}}).
 * Simplicity Condition (Example): \mathbf{H(X)} is simple (e.g., \mathbf{H(X) \cong \mathbb{Z}_n} for small n), and its action on \mathbf{B_\pi} is transitive on at least one orbit \mathbf{\mathcal{O}(b_\pi)}.
 * Interpretation: The \mathbf{O(1)} phase is characterized by the extensional world \mathbf{B_\pi} itself experiencing macro motion, where the dynamics permutes the extensional sectors.
General \mathbf{O(c)}: Holonomy Type Classification
For \mathbf{c \geq 2}, the phase is determined by the complexity of the holonomy group \mathbf{H(X)} and its action on tensor powers \mathbf{B_\pi^{\otimes n}}, as defined by the Multi-Copy Curvature Law:
A Mark object \mathbf{X} is in Phase \mathbf{O(c)} if and only if:
 * Local Flatness: The action of \mathbf{H(X)} on \mathbf{B_\pi^{\otimes n}} is trivial (or observationally trivial) for all \mathbf{n < c}.
 * Non-Local Curvature: The action of \mathbf{H(X)} on the \mathbf{c}-fold tensor product \mathbf{B_\pi^{\otimes c}} is non-trivial.
The label \mathbf{c} is an invariant of the isomorphism class of the multi-copy action \mathbf{(B_\pi^{\otimes c}, H(X))}.

5.1.2 The Induced Monoid
Definition 5.2 (Induced Monoid K_Ï€):

               = (kâ‚‚_Ï€ âˆ˜ q) âˆ˜ kâ‚   (kâ‚‚ preserves q)

               = kâ‚‚_Ï€ âˆ˜ (kâ‚_Ï€ âˆ˜ q) (kâ‚ preserves q)
               = (kâ‚‚_Ï€ âˆ˜ kâ‚_Ï€) âˆ˜ q (associativity)
